Causality and Created Things
advanced
5 min
Cause in a Part and the Contradiction Objection
Al-Iji defends the permissibility of contradictory causes in different parts without transfer of judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical objection that if a cause in a part transfers its judgment to the whole, contradictory attributes would arise, and the response that the contradiction only appears if the transfer is assumed.

Core Idea
Contradiction arises only if judgments transfer, not from separate causes in parts.

This passage from al-Iji's work addresses a key objection in causal reasoning: if a cause resides in a part and its effect characterizes the whole, then contradictory attributes in different parts would force the whole to be contradictory. Al-Iji defends the possibility by separating the existence of causes from the transfer of their judgments.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector claims that if the cause's judgment transfers from part to whole, then knowledge in one part and ignorance in another would make the whole both knowledgeable and ignorant, an impossibility.
Notice how the objector uses this to deny the premise of transfer.
2
Identify the response
The author responds by denying that the coexistence of knowledge and ignorance in different parts is impossible in itself, if we ignore the transfer of judgments.
Key phrase: 'if we disregard the transfer'.
3
Understand the logical structure
The objector's argument is a conditional syllogism assuming the transfer. The author's refutation shows that the impossibility only follows if the transfer is assumed, which is the very point under dispute.
This is a circular argument.
4
Grasp the resolution
The author concludes that the subsistence of opposite causes in different parts is possible in itself; the contradiction only arises if their judgments transfer to the whole.
The distinction between cause in part and judgment on whole is crucial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و قيام العلة بجزء لو أوجب الحكم للكل كما ذهبتم إليه لزم كون الكل عالماً جاهلاً) معاً، إذا قام العلم بجزء منه، (و) قام ( الجهل بآخر، لا يقال : هذا ) أي قيام العلم بجزء مع قيام الجهل بجزء آخر ( تقدير محال لتضادهما ) أي لتضاد العلم والجهل ) باعتبار تضاد حكميهما ) ، أعني العالمية والجاهلية، فإذا قام العلم بجزء لم يجز قيام الجهل بجزء آخر، وإلا كان الكل عالماً وجاهلاً معاً؛ (لأنا نقول : أنه ) يعني قيام العلم بجزء والجهل بآخر (جائز) لذاته ؛ فإنا إذا قطعنا النظر عن تعدي حكمي العلم والجهل من الجزء إلى الكل كان قيام كل منهما بجزء منه أمراً ممكناً لا امتناع
،
قوله : ( وقيام العلة بجزء إلخ إثبات لكلية المقدمة الممنوعة، أعني امتناع القيام بمحل آخر بضم مقدمات أخر يبطل كون محل العلة جزءاً لمحل الحكم.
قوله : ( إذا قام العلم (بجزء أي العلم التصديقي بشيء معين في وقت، وقام الجهل المركب بذلك الشيء المعين بجزء آخر في ذلك الوقت، وإنما قيد الجهل بالمركب ليكون العلة معنی موجوداً، واعتبر اتحاد المتعلق والوقت إذ لا استحالة في كون شخص عالماً وجاهلاً بالقياس إلى شيئين ولا في وقتين كمن اعتقد قيام زيد في وقت ثم اعتقد أنه ليس بقائم في وقت آخر، والحال أنه قائم في الوقتين. قوله : (لا يقال هذا إلخ منع لبطلان التالي بسند أنه لازم على تقدير محال، وهو قيام العلم والجهل بجزئين معاً، والمحال يجوز أن يستلزم المحال . قوله : ( لتضادهما إلخ) والمانع وإن كفاه مجرد جواز كونه تقدير محال، إلا أنه لما كان ادعاؤه من غير دليل عليه مكابرة؛ لاطراده في كل قياس استثنائي يستثنى عنه نقيض التالي، أيده بأن بينهما تضاداً باعتبار تضاد الحكمين بناء على المفروض المتنازع فيه، وهو عدم تعدي الحكم عن محل العلة .
قوله : (جائز لذاته يعني أنه ممكن في ذاته، فعلى تقدير وقوعه لو تعدى حكمهما إلى الكل يلزم اجتماع الضدين.
قوله: (أمراً ممكناً) إن أراد أنه على تقدير قطع النظر عن التعدي يكون قيام كل منهما ممكناً في نفس الأمر فممنوع، وإن أراد أنه على ذلك التقدير يكون ممكناً عند التعقل حيث لم يحكم العقل بامتناعه فمسلم ، لكن لا يجدي نفعاً، لأنه لا بد من إمكانه في نفس الأمر ليترتب عليه لزوم المحال في نفس الأم
And if the subsistence of the cause in a part necessitated the judgment for the whole, as you have held, then it would be necessary that the whole be knowledgeable and ignorant simultaneously, if knowledge subsisted in one part of it and ignorance in another. It cannot be said: this (i.e., the subsistence of knowledge in one part together with the subsistence of ignorance in another part) is an impossible supposition because of their opposition, i.e., the opposition of knowledge and ignorance, in consideration of the opposition of their two judgments, namely knowledge-hood and ignorance-hood. For if knowledge subsists in a part, it is not permissible for ignorance to subsist in another part, otherwise the whole would be knowledgeable and ignorant together. (But we say: that), i.e., the subsistence of knowledge in a part and ignorance in another, (is permissible) in itself; for if we disregard the transfer of the judgments of knowledge and ignorance from the part to the whole, then the subsistence of each in a part of it is something possible, not impossible. His saying: ('And the subsistence of the cause in a part, etc.') is a proof for the universality of the denied premise, namely the impossibility of subsistence in another locus, by adding other premises that invalidate the locus of the cause being a part of the locus of the judgment. His saying: ('If knowledge subsists in a part, i.e., assenting knowledge of a specific thing at a time, and compound ignorance of that specific thing subsists in another part at that same time. He restricted ignorance to compound ignorance so that the cause would be something existent, and he considered the unity of the object and time, since there is no impossibility in a person being knowledgeable and ignorant with respect to two things nor in two times, like someone who believes Zayd is standing at one time then believes he is not standing at another time, while in fact he is standing at both times. His saying: ('It cannot be said this, etc.') is a denial of the falsity of the consequent on the ground that it is necessary upon an impossible supposition, which is the subsistence of knowledge and ignorance in two parts together, and the impossible may necessitate the impossible. His saying: ('Because of their opposition, etc.') And the objector, even if the mere possibility of it being an impossible supposition suffices him, yet since his claim without proof for it is obstinacy, because it is pervasive in every conditional syllogism from which the contradictory of the consequent is excluded, he supported it by saying that there is an opposition between them in consideration of the opposition of the two judgments, based on the disputed assumption, which is the non-transfer of the judgment from the locus of the cause. His saying: ('is permissible in itself') means it is possible in itself. So upon the supposition of its occurrence, if their two judgments transferred to the whole, the joining of two opposites would follow. His saying: ('something possible') – if he means that upon the supposition of disregarding the transfer, the subsistence of each is possible in actual fact, then it is denied; and if he means that upon that supposition it is possible in the mind, since reason does not judge its impossibility, then it is granted, but it does not benefit, because it must be possible in actual fact for the necessity of the impossible to follow in actual fact [؟].
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is an objection and response concerning the claim that if the cause (al-'illa) subsists in a part of the subject, and the judgment of that cause transfers to the whole, then contradictory judgments could arise. The objector argues: if the cause (e.g., knowledge) subsists in one part, and another cause (e.g., ignorance) subsists in another part, then the whole would be both knowledgeable and ignorant, which is impossible. Therefore, the premise that a cause's judgment transfers from part to whole is false. The author responds by distinguishing between the subsistence of the causes themselves and the transfer of their judgments. If we ignore the transfer (ta'diya) of the judgments from the part to the whole, then there is no contradiction in having knowledge in one part and ignorance in another; the parts are separate and can each have their own attribute. The impossibility only arises if the judgments transfer to the whole. The objector's claim that the coexistence of knowledge and ignorance in different parts is impossible in itself is denied. The commentator elaborates: knowledge and ignorance can coexist in different parts of a single entity as long as the judgments do not apply to the whole. To avoid a trivial objection, the case is specified to assenting knowledge and compound ignorance of the same object at the same time, but even then, if the judgments are confined to the parts, there is no problem. The objector's argument that such coexistence is an impossible supposition because it leads to the whole being both knowledgeable and ignorant is only valid if the judgments transfer. Since the very point at issue is whether judgments transfer, this reasoning is circular. The commentator also notes that the objector's claim that knowledge and ignorance are opposed by virtue of their judgments (knowledge-hood and ignorance-hood) is based on the assumption that the judgments transfer; thus it begs the question. The response concludes that the subsistence of knowledge in one part and ignorance in another is possible in itself, and only if the judgments transfer would a contradiction arise. Therefore, the original claim that the cause's judgment transfers to the whole does not stand.

Key Terms
قيام العلة بجزء (subsistence of the cause in a part)
تعدي الحكم (transfer of judgment)
علم (knowledge)
جهل (ignorance)
تضاد (opposition)
تقدير محال (impossible supposition)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Coexistence of opposite attributes (like knowledge and ignorance) in different parts of a whole is inherently impossible.
Correction: The impossibility only arises if the judgments of those attributes transfer to the whole. If the judgments remain confined to the parts, there is no contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the coexistence of knowledge and ignorance in different parts of a whole permissible?
A) Because knowledge and ignorance are not really opposites
B) Because the parts are separate and the judgments do not transfer to the whole
C) Because the whole cannot be both knowledgeable and ignorant
D) Because the objector's argument is based on an impossible supposition

Key Takeaway
Coexistence of opposite causes in different parts is possible as long as their judgments do not transfer to the whole.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of an area of your life where you hold two seemingly contradictory beliefs about yourself (e.g., 'I am patient' in some situations and 'I am impatient' in others). Do you instinctively feel you must be one or the other overall? How can the lesson's distinction between parts and whole help you embrace your complexity without inner contradiction? What specific step will you take this week to accept that you can be both without labeling your entire self?
?
Recall a recent disagreement where you dismissed someone's argument because you found a flaw in one part. Did you assume that the flaw invalidated their whole position? Reflect on how the objector in this lesson committed that very error. How can you practice evaluating parts separately before judging the whole? Commit to one concrete change in how you listen next time you disagree.
?
The objector held an assumption (that cause in a part transfers judgment) so firmly that he saw a contradiction where there was none. What is one deep-rooted assumption you hold about yourself, others, or God that might be causing you to see impossibilities that are not truly there? What would it mean for you to 'disregard the transfer' and re-examine that assumption with fresh eyes? Write down one action you will take to challenge that assumption this week.

Causality and Created Things
intermediate

Objection and Response on Knowledge and Ignorance in Parts
Presents the objection that a cause's locus being a part necessitates contradictory predicates on the whole, and the response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that a cause's locus in a part would lead to contradictory predicates on the whole, and the response that the simultaneous subsistence of opposite causes in different parts is possible if the transfer of their rulings to the whole is disregarded.

Core Idea
A cause's subsistence in a part may not transfer its ruling to the whole.

This passage presents a dialectical exchange about causality. The objector argues that if a cause's ruling transfers from a part to the whole, then contradictory rulings would arise. The responder defends the possibility by distinguishing between the subsistence of causes and the transfer of their rulings.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Focus on the conditional statement: if a cause subsists in a part and its ruling transfers to the whole, then knowledge in one part and ignorance in another would make the whole both knowing and ignorant.
Note that the objector uses this to deny that a cause can be in a part if its ruling transfers.
2
Identify the response
The responder argues that the simultaneous subsistence of knowledge and ignorance in different parts is possible in itself, if we disregard the transfer of their rulings to the whole.
The key is the distinction between subsistence and transfer.
3
Understand the conditions
Note the stipulations: knowledge is assent-based, ignorance is compound, same object and time. This avoids irrelevant cases.
Without these conditions, the scenario might not be problematic.

Source Text

(The cause's subsistence in a part, if it necessitated the ruling for the whole as you claim, would necessitate that the whole is both knowing and ignorant) together, if knowledge subsists in a part of it, and ignorance subsists in another part. It cannot be said: This—i.e., the subsistence of knowledge in a part together with the subsistence of ignorance in another part—(is an impossible supposition because of their mutual opposition) i.e., the opposition of knowledge and ignorance, in consideration of the opposition of their two rulings, namely being knowing and being ignorant. For if knowledge subsists in a part, it is not permissible for ignorance to subsist in another part; otherwise the whole would be both knowing and ignorant together. (For we say: That) i.e., the subsistence of knowledge in a part and ignorance in another (is permissible in itself. For if we disregard the transfer of the rulings of knowledge and ignorance from the part to the whole, then the subsistence of each of them in a part is a possible matter, not impossible.) His statement: (The cause's subsistence in a part etc.) is a proof of the universality of the denied premise, namely the impossibility of subsistence in another locus, by adding other premises that invalidate the locus of the cause being a part of the locus of the ruling. His statement: (If knowledge subsists in a part) i.e., assent-based knowledge of a specific thing at a time, and compound ignorance of that same specific thing subsists in another part at that same time. He restricted ignorance to compound ignorance so that the cause is an existing meaning, and he stipulated unity of object and time, since there is no impossibility in a person being knowing and ignorant with respect to two things or at two times, like someone who believes Zayd is standing at one time and then believes he is not standing at another time, while he is actually standing at both times. His statement: (It cannot be said: This etc.) is a prevention of the falsity of the consequent, on the grounds that it is necessary upon an impossible supposition, namely the simultaneous subsistence of knowledge and ignorance in two parts, and the impossible may entail the impossible. His statement: (Because of their mutual opposition etc.) For the objector, though the mere possibility of its being an impossible supposition might suffice, yet since claiming this without proof is obstinacy—because it would apply to every exceptive syllogism where the contradictory of the consequent is excepted—he supported it by saying there is opposition between them due to the opposition of the two rulings, based on the assumed point under dispute, which is the non-transfer of the ruling from the locus of the cause. His statement: (Permissible in itself) means it is possible in itself; so if it actually occurred, then if their rulings transferred to the whole, it would necessitate the conjunction of two opposites. His statement: (A possible matter) If he means that, upon disregarding transfer, the subsistence of each is possible in reality, then it is denied; if he means that upon that supposition it is possible in the intellect, since the intellect does not judge it impossible, then it is granted, but it is of no use, because its possibility in reality is necessary for the entailment of impossibility in reality to follow from it.

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and response within the discussion of causality. The objection states: If a cause subsists in a part of a whole, and the ruling of the cause transfers to the whole, then if knowledge subsists in one part and ignorance in another part, the whole would be both knowing and ignorant simultaneously—which is impossible. Therefore, the cause cannot be in a part if its ruling transfers. The response: The opponent cannot simply dismiss this as an impossible supposition due to the opposition of knowledge and ignorance, because the objector claims impossibility based on the transfer of rulings. However, the response argues that the simultaneous subsistence of knowledge and ignorance in different parts is possible in itself if we disregard transfer. The transfers of rulings (knowledge and ignorance) are what would cause the contradiction, not the mere subsistence. The commentary clarifies the conditions: knowledge is assent-based, ignorance is compound, with same object and time, to avoid irrelevant scenarios. It also explains that the objector's attempt to reject the supposition as impossible is not valid without proof, and even if it were an impossible supposition, it could still entail another impossibility. The final commentary note discusses whether the possibility is in reality or in intellect, concluding that only real possibility would support the argument.

Key Terms
قيام العلة
حكم
تعدية
علم
جهل
تقدير محال
تضاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the objection is about the impossibility of knowledge and ignorance coexisting in the same subject, but the real issue is whether the transfer of the ruling from part to whole is what causes the impossibility.
Correction: The passage shows that the simultaneous subsistence of knowledge and ignorance in different parts is not impossible in itself; the impossibility arises only when their rulings are transferred to the whole.

Checkpoint
What is the response to the objection that knowledge in one part and ignorance in another would make the whole both knowing and ignorant?
A) The simultaneous subsistence of knowledge and ignorance in different parts is impossible in itself.
B) The simultaneous subsistence is possible if we disregard transfer of rulings to the whole.
C) The whole cannot be both knowing and ignorant under any circumstance.
D) The objection is valid and accepted.

Key Takeaway
The key point is that the impossibility of contradictory rulings arises only when the rulings are transferred to the whole; the mere coexistence of opposite causes in parts is not inherently impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you catch yourself in a moment of ignorance (e.g., misunderstanding something important), do you let that one part define your whole identity as 'ignorant'? How would it change your self-perception if you saw that part as separate from the whole, as this lesson suggests?
?
Think of someone you have judged entirely based on one flaw. Is it possible that their flaw subsists in only a part of them, while other parts hold knowledge or virtue? Will you now treat them differently, refusing to transfer the one ruling to their entire person?
?
Your own heart can hold both faith and doubt in different 'parts' at the same time. How does the possibility of these coexisting without transferring their rulings to your whole soul free you from despair and motivate you to nourish the part where knowledge resides?

Power and Incapacity: Extending the Objection
Extends the objection with power/incapacity example and refutes the universal rule claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objector extends the transfer argument to power and incapacity in different parts of a person, and why the response depends on considering incapacity as an existing meaning.

Core Idea
Power and incapacity in one person challenge the transfer rule.

The objector intensifies the debate by presenting a concrete example from everyday life: a person may have power to move in one hand and incapacity in the other. This seems to force a contradiction if each attribute's ruling transfers to the whole. But the response clarifies that this only works if one views incapacity as a real opposite of power.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection example
Read the Arabic passage focusing on the example of power and incapacity in two hands. Notice how the objector claims it is an actual occurrence, not a hypothetical.
The example is meant to be from common experience.
2
Identify the conditional response
Find where the commentator says the response only works against those who view incapacity as an existing meaning. Understand that this limits the objection's force.
3
Examine the refutation of the counter-objection
Look at how the author refutes the idea that a particular example cannot validate a universal rule. Note the claim that the impossibility of transfer is necessary and the example is just clarification.
This part defends the use of concrete examples in kalam.

Source Text

قوله : ( وأيضاً ) : ما ذكرتموه إنما يتأتى في العلم والجهل، لا في جميع العلل التي جوزتم تعدية أحكامها، فقد تقوم القدرة على تحريك جسم بيد ) من شخص، ( والعجز عنه ( بأخرى، فيجب اتصاف الجملة بهما ) منه قياماً معلوماً بالضرورة، فلو جاز تعدي الحكم إلى الكل لكان ذلك الشخص قادراً على تحريكه، وعاجزاً عنه معاً، وليس يمكن أن يقال : هذا تقدير محال، لأنه واقع بلا ريبة إلا أن هذا الجواب إنما ينتهض على القائلين بأن العجز معنى موجود مضاد للقدرة، وقولهم : إن المثال الجزئي لا يصحح القاعدة الكلية مدفوع بأن امتناع تعدي الحكم عن محل الصفة ضروري والتمثيل للتوضيح ولم يذكره المصنف لأنه مر مثله في بحث الوجود وشرع في جواب الإلزامات التي ذكرها الأستاذ بقوله : ( وأما الفعل فلا يوجب لمحله حكماً ثبوتياً؛ لأن الفاعلية صفة اعتبارية ) ولا العلم ونحوه ) يوجب لمتعلقه ) حكماً ، ( وإلا كان للمعدوم) الممتنع (صفة ثبوتية)، إذا تعلق العلم به. قوله : (وقولهم إلخ) اعتذار عن ترك التعرض للجواب عن الاعتراض الثاني مع التعرض للجواب عما يوضحه. قوله : ( بأن امتناع تعدي الحكم إلخ ) هذا الحكم أخص من المدعى لأن المراد منه امتناع تعدي الحكم عن محل قام به الصفة كعالمية زيد بعلم عمرو، والمدعى امتناع تعدي الحكم عن محل العلة مطلقا سواء كان له محل أو لا ولذا تعرض في الاحتجاج لنفي كون العلة قائمة بنفسها فما قيل: إن دعوى الضرورة ينافي الاحتجاج وهم . قوله : ( والتمثيل للتوضيح لا للإثبات فالمناقشة بأنه لا يصحح القاعدة الكلية مكابرة . قوله : ( لأنه مر مثله إلخ ) حيث إنه ذكر قال بعض الفضلاء : إن اشتراك الوجود بديهي، ومنعه مكابرة وللتفاوت بينهما في البيان واتحادهما في المقصود زاد لفظ : مثله . قوله : (صفة اعتبارية) إذ لو كانت موجودة لزم تسلسل الفاعليات . قوله: (حكماً) أي ثبوتياً . قوله: فلا يوجب لمحله حكماً قيل : الأولى أن يترك لفظ لمحله لأن ظاهر متمسك الأستاذ أن الفعل يوجب عندكم لغير محله حكماً ثبوتياً، كما لا يخفى. اللهم إلا أن يكون مراده أن الفعل لا يوجب لمحله حكماً ثبوتياً فضلاً عن أن يفيده لغير محله . قوله : ( لأن الفاعلية صفة اعتبارية ) أي غير ثابتة في الخارج لا أنها غير موجودة فيه، إذ لا ينافي كونها حكماً ثبوتياً.
His statement: (And also:) What you mentioned only applies to knowledge and ignorance, not to all causes whose rulings you allow to transfer. For power to move a body may subsist in the hand of a person, and incapacity for it in another hand, so the whole must be described by both—this being known necessarily. So if the transfer of the ruling to the whole were permissible, that person would be both powerful to move it and incapable of it together. And it cannot be said: This is an impossible supposition, because it is actual without doubt. Except that this answer only stands against those who say that incapacity is an existing meaning opposed to power. And their saying that the particular example does not validate the universal rule is refuted because the impossibility of transferring a ruling from the locus of an attribute is necessary, and the example is for clarification. And the author did not mention it because its like passed in the discussion of existence, and he proceeded with the answer to the obligations (ilzamat) that the master mentioned, by his saying: (As for action, it does not necessitate for its locus a positive ruling; because agenthood is an attributive concept) and neither knowledge nor the like necessitates for its object a ruling, (otherwise the nonexistent) the impossible (would have a positive attribute) if knowledge is connected to it. His statement: (And their saying etc.) is an apology for not engaging with the answer to the second objection while engaging with the answer to what clarifies it. His statement: (That the impossibility of transferring the ruling etc.) This ruling is more specific than the claim, because what is meant is the impossibility of transferring the ruling from a locus in which the attribute subsists, like Zayd's being knowing by 'Amr's knowledge, while the claim is the impossibility of transferring the ruling from the locus of the cause absolutely, whether it has a locus or not. Hence in the argument he engaged with negating that the cause subsists by itself; so what is said that the claim of necessity contradicts the argument is a delusion. His statement: (And the example is for clarification, not for proof, so the objection that it does not validate the universal rule is obstinacy.) His statement: (Because its like passed etc.) Since he mentioned, 'Some virtuous ones said: The commonality of existence is self-evident, and its denial is obstinacy,' and because of the difference between them in explanation and their unity in purpose, he added the word 'like'. His statement: (An attributive concept) because if it were existent, an infinite regress of agenthoods would follow. His statement: (A ruling) i.e., positive. His statement: (So it does not necessitate for its locus a ruling) It is said: It would be better to drop the word 'for its locus' because the apparent meaning of the master's argument is that action necessitates for other than its locus a positive ruling, as is not hidden. However, unless he means that action does not necessitate for its locus a positive ruling, let alone confer it for other than its locus. His statement: (Because agenthood is an attributive concept) i.e., not established in the external world, not that it is non-existent in it, for that does not contradict its being a positive ruling.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage continues the objection-and-response sequence. The objector extends the argument by noting that it is not limited to knowledge and ignorance; the same problem occurs with power and incapacity in different parts of a person (e.g., power in one hand, incapacity in another). If the ruling transfers from part to whole, the person would be both powerful and incapable of the same action, which is actual (we observe people with such conditions), not an impossible supposition. The response is that this objection only works if one holds that incapacity is an existing meaning opposed to power (as opposed to merely the absence of power). The commentator then addresses a potential counter: the particular example does not prove the general rule, but replies that the impossibility of transfer is necessary, and the example is illustrative. The passage then transitions to the author's final argument (from the 'ustadh'): action does not confer a positive ruling upon its locus because agenthood is an attributive concept (i'tibari), not a real attribute. Similarly, knowledge and other attributes do not confer a positive ruling on their objects; otherwise, even impossible non-existents would have positive attributes if known. The commentary elaborates on the meaning of 'attributive concept', clarifying it means it is not a real externality, but that does not preclude it from being a positive ruling in a sense.

عجز
تعدية الحكم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the example is a hypothetical impossibility, but the objector insists it is actual.
Correction: The passage states it is 'actual without doubt' (waqi' bila riyba), so it is a real scenario.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, the response to the power/incapacity example only stands against those who believe that incapacity is:
A) The mere absence of power
B) An existing meaning opposed to power
C) A non-existent concept
D) A subset of knowledge

Key Takeaway
The objector's example challenges the transfer rule, but its force depends on considering incapacity as a real attribute, not a mere absence.

?

?
This passage shows that not every objection is valid unless certain conditions are met. Think of a time you accepted a doubt without checking its foundations. How will you respond differently next time?
?
The objector's example of power and incapacity forces you to consider part-whole relationships. In your own life, are you consistent in how you apply principles to yourself and others, or do you make exceptions? Ask Allah for tawfiq to be just.

Action Confers No Positive Ruling: Agenthood as Attributive Concept
Presents the final argument that action confers no positive ruling because agenthood is an attributive concept.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why action does not confer a positive ruling upon its locus, and why agenthood is considered an attributive concept (i'tibari) rather than a real attribute.

Core Idea
Agenthood is conceptual, not real, so action confers no positive ruling.

After addressing objections, the author presents his final argument: action does not necessitate a positive ruling on its locus. Agenthood is an attributive concept, meaning it is not a real external attribute. This prevents an infinite regress and avoids attributing positive qualities to impossible non-existents. The passage also clarifies that 'attributive concept' does not mean non-existent; it still constitutes a positive ruling in a sense.

Guided Path
1
Read the main argument
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'As for action...' to understand the claim: action does not necessitate a positive ruling for its locus because agenthood is an attributive concept.
This is the author's decisive refutation.
2
Consider the analogy with knowledge
Notice how the argument uses knowledge as an analogy: knowledge does not confer a positive ruling on its object, otherwise even impossible non-existents would have a positive attribute.
This shows the principle is general.
3
Understand the clarification on 'attributive concept'
The commentary explains that 'attributive concept' means not established in the external world, but it is not non-existent. This subtlety is important to avoid misunderstanding.

?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
5 min
Action Does Not Necessitate a Positive Judgment for Its Locus
Al-Iji states that action does not necessitate a positive attribute for its locus because agenthood is relative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why action and knowledge do not impose positive attributes on their loci or objects, and how the relative nature of agenthood prevents attributing real qualities to non-existents.

Core Idea
Agenthood is a relative attribute, not a real quality.

This lesson examines a crucial distinction in kalam: the difference between actions and real attributes. Al-Iji clarifies that when we say something acts, we are not saying that the object of that action gains a real attribute. Instead, agenthood is merely a relation. This reasoning extends to knowledge: if knowing something gave it a real quality, then even impossible non-existents would have attributes when known, which is absurd. This passage helps us avoid conflating relational judgments with inherent properties.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic sentence carefully. Note the structure: 'وأما الفعل فلا يوجب...' and the reason given with 'لأن'. Then read the translation.
Pay attention to the connective 'وإلا' which introduces the reductio ad absurdum.
2
Identify the key terms
Identify the five key terms: الفعل, الفاعلية, صفة اعتبارية, الحكم الثبوتي, المعدوم الممتنع. Understand their meanings in context.
صفة اعتبارية is crucial — it means a relative or relational attribute, not a real attribute inhering in a subject.
3
Analyze the argument structure
Break down the reasoning: The claim is that action does not necessitate a positive judgment for its locus. The proof is that agenthood is relative. Then an analogy with knowledge: knowledge does not impose a judgment on its object, otherwise impossible non-existents would have positive attributes. This is a reductio ad absurdum.
Consider why the impossible non-existent is a problem: it is nothing, so attributing anything positive to it is contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الفعل فلا يوجب لمحله حكماً ثبوتياً؛ لأن الفاعلية صفة اعتبارية، ولا العلم ونحوه يوجب لمتعلقه حكماً، وإلا كان للمعدوم الممتنع صفة ثبوتية إذا تعلق العلم به.
As for action, it does not necessitate a positive judgment for its locus, because agenthood is a relative attribute. And knowledge and the like do not necessitate a judgment for their object; otherwise, the impossible non-existent would have a positive attribute if knowledge is related to it.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage explains that action (الفعل) does not cause a positive attribute (حكم ثبوتي) for its place or subject. The reason is that agenthood (الفاعلية) is a relative attribute (صفة اعتبارية) — it is not a real quality inhering in the agent but a relation. Similarly, knowledge and similar mental acts do not impose a positive attribute on their objects. If they did, then impossible non-existents (معدوم ممتنع) would have positive attributes when known, which is absurd.

Key Terms
الفعل
الفاعلية
صفة اعتبارية
الحكم الثبوتي
المعدوم الممتنع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'agent' implies a real quality of being an agent.
Correction: The text clarifies agenthood is merely a relational attribute, not a real quality.

Checkpoint
Why does action not necessitate a positive judgment for its locus?
A) Because action is nonexistent
B) Because agenthood is a relative attribute
C) Because the locus is not affected
D) Because knowledge is more important

Key Takeaway
Action and knowledge do not confer real attributes on their objects; they are merely relational judgments.

The Cause Must Be Existent: Proof by Necessity
Al-Iji states the third issue and gives the proof by necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational necessity that a positive effect requires a positive cause.

Core Idea
A positive judgment cannot come from pure nothingness.

This passage addresses a fundamental question: can something come from nothing? Al-Iji states the consensus that the cause of a positive attribute must be existent. He presents the proof that this is a matter of rational necessity, not empirical observation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the claim of consensus and the mention of different methods.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the key claim
Al-Iji states that there is agreement (اتفاق) that the cause is existent, but scholars differ on how to prove it. The passage then describes one method: necessity (ضرورة).
Only one method is elaborated here.
3
Understand the necessity argument
The reasoning is that a positive judgment (حكم ثبوتي) cannot be caused by pure non-existence (عدم محض) or sheer negation. Therefore, the cause must be an existent thing (أمر وجودي).
This is a rational necessity, not empirical.
4
Reflect on the reliable method
Al-Iji endorses this as the reliable method (الطريق المعول عليه). Consider why this is considered stronger than other methods.

This passage presents the third issue regarding cause and effect: the cause must be existent. It begins by stating that there is consensus (بالإتفاق) that the cause is existent, but scholars differ in how they prove it. Then it mentions the first method: some claim it is necessary (ضرورة) — meaning it is self-evident. The reasoning is that a positive judgment (حكم ثبوتي) requires a positive cause; pure non-existence or sheer negation cannot produce a positive effect. Therefore, the cause must be an existent thing (أمر وجودي). The passage concludes by endorsing this as the reliable method (الطريق المعول عليه).

The Second Proof: The Argument from Knowledge and Ignorance and Its Answer
Al-Iji presents the second proof using knowledge and ignorance and then responds to it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that the cause must be existent, using the example of knowledge and ignorance, and the response that distinguishes between a negative attribute and the negation of an attribute.

Core Idea
Existence gives knowledge a causal power that nonexistent ignorance lacks.

This passage presents a clever argument from parity that aims to show that a nonexistent cause leads to contradiction. Al-Iji then responds by introducing a crucial distinction between a negative attribute (صفة عدمية) and the mere negation of an attribute. The discussion highlights how kalam thinkers refined their concepts of causality and attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the proof and response
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that the passage has two parts: a proof (from 'ومنهم...' to 'معاً') and a response (from 'قلنا' to the end).
The proof is an argument against the opponent, and the response is Al-Iji's answer.
2
Understand the initial argument
The proof assumes parity: if nonexistent knowledge can cause 'being-knowing', then nonexistent ignorance should cause 'being-ignorant'. If both are absent from the same locus, that locus would be both knowing and ignorant, which is contradictory.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Examine the response
Al-Iji distinguishes between the existence of a negative attribute (الصفة العدمية) and the negation of an attribute (سلب الصفة). He claims that existing knowledge can be a cause for 'being-knowing' because it has the advantage of existence, while nonexistent ignorance cannot be a cause for 'being-ignorant'.
The response does not grant the parity assumed in the proof.
4
Reflect on the key insight
The lesson is that only existents can be causes. Knowledge, being existent, can serve as a cause, whereas ignorance, being nonexistent, cannot. Thus, the proof fails because it mistakenly equates the causal efficacy of existent and nonexistent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من احتج عليه بوجوه. الأول لو جاز العالمية بعلم معدوم لزم الجاهلية بجهل معدوم؛ إذ لا مزية لأحدهما على الآخر، فإذا عدما عن محل كان ذلك المحل عالماً جاهلاً معاً. قلنا : النزاع في ثبوت الصفة العدمية، لا في سلب الصفة، فإنا ندعي أنه يجوز أن يكون العلم الموجود علة للعالمية الثانية، فلا يلزم كون الجهل علة للجاهلية لمزية العلم على الجهل من حيث الوجود فيجوز أن يكون علة بخلاف الجهل فإنه معدوم ولا يصلح علة لشيء.
And among them are those who argued for it by proofs. The first: if being knowing were allowed by a nonexistent knowledge, then being ignorant would follow by a nonexistent ignorance; for there is no advantage of one over the other. So if both (knowledge and ignorance) are absent from a locus, that locus would be both knowing and ignorant simultaneously. We say: the dispute is about the existence of a negative attribute, not about the negation of an attribute. For we claim that it is possible that existing knowledge is the cause for the second being-knowing, so it does not follow that ignorance is the cause for being-ignorant, because knowledge has an advantage over ignorance in terms of existence, so it can be a cause, unlike ignorance which is nonexistent and cannot be a cause for anything.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof that the cause must be existent, using the example of knowledge and ignorance. The argument is: if being knowing (العالمية) could be caused by a nonexistent knowledge, then by parity, being ignorant (الجاهلية) would be caused by a nonexistent ignorance. Since there is no advantage between the two nonexistents, if both are absent, the same entity would be both knowing and ignorant — a contradiction. Therefore, knowledge must be existent to cause being-knowing. The answer (قلنا) is that the dispute is about the existence of a negative attribute (صفة عدمية) not about the negation of an attribute. The response clarifies that existing knowledge can be a cause for being-knowing, while nonexistent ignorance cannot be a cause for being-ignorant because a cause must be existent.

Key Terms
العالمية
الجاهلية
علم معدوم
جهل معدوم
الصفة العدمية
سلب الصفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the proof assumes parity between knowledge and ignorance, and that the response fails to address it.
Correction: The response shows that existence gives knowledge a special status, so parity does not hold: knowledge can be a cause when existent, while ignorance, being nonexistent, cannot.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the response to the second proof?
A) Ignorance can also be a cause
B) Existing knowledge can be a cause while nonexistent ignorance cannot
C) Both ignorance and knowledge are nonexistent
D) The argument is invalid

Key Takeaway
The second proof fails because it equates knowledge and ignorance as nonexistent causes, but the response shows that only an existent can be a cause, so existing knowledge can cause being-knowing while nonexistent ignorance cannot.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you rely on future hopes or past regrets as if they have causal power over your present actions, are you treating nonexistents as causes? How can you shift your focus to what exists now—your current intentions and actions—as the only real cause of change?

This passage teaches that only an existent can be a cause. In your relationship with God, do you ever treat your own nonexistent efforts (like 'I'll pray later' or 'I'll repent tomorrow') as if they already have effect? What would it look like to make your existing faith and obedience the real cause of your spiritual growth starting today?

The objector assumed that knowledge and ignorance are symmetrical when absent. Where in your life have you assumed that two things are equal simply because they are both absent—like comparing two missed opportunities or two unfulfilled duties? The Cause is Existential: Proof by Necessity
The author states that the cause is existent by consensus and gives the proof by necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof from necessity that the cause of any positive judgment must be existent, and why pure non-being cannot serve as a cause.

Core Idea
A positive effect cannot come from pure nothingness.

In this passage, the author tackles a fundamental principle of causality: what kind of thing can be a cause? He argues that by sheer rational necessity, any cause that brings about a positive state of affairs must itself be existent. This might seem obvious, but it carries deep implications for how we think about creation and dependence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own phrasing come through.
Notice the terms 'العدم المحض' and 'النفي الصرف'.
2
Identify the key claim and its proof
The claim is that the cause must be existent. The proof is that a positive judgment cannot be caused by pure non-being or absolute negation.
Ask yourself: why can't non-being be a cause?
3
Reflect on why this is the 'relied-upon way'
The author states this proof is soundest. Consider why this argument might be considered necessary and evident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage defines the third issue in the discussion of cause and effect: that the cause (العلة) is existent (وجودية) according to all scholars, though they differ in the method of demonstrating that it is existent. The author then presents the first method: the proof by necessity (طريق الضرورة). The argument is that any positive judgment, i.e., a judgment that something is affirmed, cannot be caused by pure non-being or absolute negation, because that would be absurd. Therefore, the cause must be an existent thing (أمر وجودي). The author notes that this is the soundest method.

The Second Proof: The Argument from Knowledge and Ignorance
The author presents the argument from knowledge and ignorance and then refines it with a distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof against a non-existent cause and the critical distinction between a privative attribute and a mere negation.

Core Idea
A non-existent cause leads to contradiction; the real issue is about privative attributes, not negations.

This lesson presents a second objection to the claim that a cause can be non-existent. The objection uses the example of knowledge and ignorance to show that accepting a non-existent cause leads to impossible contradictions. The author then responds by drawing a crucial distinction that resolves the difficulty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the English translation. Try to grasp the flow of the argument without analyzing details yet.
Note the structure: first the proof, then the response.
2
Understand the proof by analogy
The proof argues: if non-existent knowledge can make a locus 'knowing', then by parity non-existent ignorance would make it 'ignorant'. Both are non-existent, so no preference.
Why would both being absent lead to a contradiction?
3
Examine the conclusion of the proof
If both knowledge and ignorance are absent from a locus, the proof claims that locus would be both knowing and ignorant simultaneously—an impossibility.
This result is meant to show the absurdity of allowing a non-existent cause.
4
Analyze the response
The author responds by distinguishing between a privative attribute (صفة عدمية) and a negation of attribute (سلب). The dispute is about whether a privative attribute can cause a positive judgment, not about a negation causing the judgment of the attribute itself.
This distinction blocks the analogy: the proof conflates affirmation of a privative attribute with negation of an attribute.
5
Reflect on the key distinction
Why is the distinction so important? Because it clarifies that the objectors are really talking about negation, which is clearly false when applied to causality, whereas the defenders of non-existent cause are speaking of privative attributes.
This distinction safeguards the possibility of privative attributes as causes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( ومنهم من احتج عليه بوجوه. الأول لو جاز العالمية بعلم معدوم لزم الجاهلية بجهل معدوم )؛ إذ لا مزية لأحدهما على الآخر، (فإذا عدما ) أي العلم والجهل ( عن محل كان ذلك المحل عالماً جاهلاً) معاً . ( قلنا : النزاع في ثبوت الصفة العدمية، لا في سلب الصفة، فإنا ندعي أنه يجوز أن يتصف محل بصفة عدمية ويكون ذلك موجباً لحكم ثبوتي في ذلك المحل، لا أنه يجوز أن تسلب صفة عن محل ويكون ذلك السلب موجباً له حكم تلك الصفة، فإنه ظاهر البطلان .
(And among them is one who argued against it with proofs. The first: if it were permissible for worldliness (العالمية) to be caused by non-existent knowledge, then ignorance (الجاهلية) would be necessitated by non-existent ignorance; there is no superiority of one over the other. (So if both are absent) i.e., knowledge and ignorance (from a locus, that locus would be both knowing and ignorant simultaneously). (We said: The dispute concerns the affirmation of a privative attribute (الصفة العدمية), not the negation of the attribute. For we claim that it is permissible for a locus to be described by a privative attribute and that description be the cause of a positive judgment in that locus, not that it is permissible for an attribute to be negated from a locus and that negation cause it to have the judgment of that attribute, for that is clearly false.)
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof that the cause must be existent, and then gives a response. The proof is an argument by analogy: if we allow that a non-existent knowledge could be the cause for a locus to be described as 'knowing' (عالم), then by parity of reasoning, a non-existent ignorance would be the cause for that locus to be described as 'ignorant' (جاهل). Since both are non-existent, there is no superiority of one over the other. Thus, if both are absent from a locus, that locus would be both knowing and ignorant at the same time, which is impossible. The response refines the objection: the issue is about the affirmation of a privative attribute (صفة عدمية) being the cause of a positive judgment, not about the negation of an attribute causing that judgment. The objectors are actually discussing negation, not affirmation, and that is clearly false. The author distinguishes between a privative attribute (absence of attribute) and a negation of attribute (salb). A privative attribute is a real description even though it is not existent, while a negation is not a description at all. So the opponent's example conflates these two.

Key Terms
العالمية
الجاهلية
علم معدوم
جهل معدوم
الصفة العدمية
سلب الصفة
حكم ثبوتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a privative attribute (صفة عدمية) is allowed as a cause, then any negation of an attribute could also be a cause, leading to contradictions.
Correction: The author clarifies that the dispute is only about affirming a privative attribute, not about negating an attribute. A negation of an attribute is not a description at all, so it cannot cause a positive judgment.

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction the author makes in response to the proof from knowledge and ignorance?
A) Between necessary and contingent existents
B) Between a privative attribute and a negation of an attribute
C) Between worldly and spiritual knowledge
D) Between the cause and the effect

Key Takeaway
The passage establishes that allowing a non-existent cause leads to contradictions unless one distinguishes between a privative attribute and a mere negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Impossibility of Coexisting Absences: Refined Objection on Knowledge and Ignorance
The author refines the objection by arguing that knowledge and ignorance are opposites and cannot both be absent, and further analyzes the composite ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the absence of knowledge and the absence of ignorance cannot coexist, and how the distinction between simple and composite ignorance addresses a key objection in the debate on privative causation.

Core Idea
Knowledge and ignorance are opposites; the absence of one implies the presence of the other.

In this lesson, we continue the response to the objection that a privative attribute could cause a positive state. The opponent tries to use ignorance as an example, arguing that absence of knowledge (ignorance) could cause ignorance. But the author shows that knowledge and ignorance are mutually exclusive opposites like day and night—they cannot both be absent from the same locus. This leads to a refined analysis of different types of ignorance and their logical relations.

Guided Path
1
Understand the key opposition
The passage begins by stating that absence of knowledge is ignorance, and absence of ignorance is knowledge. This establishes that knowledge and ignorance are opposites (تضاد وتناف), so they cannot both be absent from the same thing.
Notice that the author uses both 'opposition' (تضاد) and 'conflict' (تناف) to emphasize mutual exclusivity.
2
Follow the opponent's reformulation
The opponent reformulates the argument by considering composite ignorance (الجهل المركب), claiming that if non-existent knowledge can cause worldliness, then non-existent composite ignorance can cause ignorance. This leads to the possibility of both absences coexisting.
Composite ignorance is a positive belief in something false, not a mere absence.
3
Analyze the response: conditional types
The response argues that even if we concede the opponent's premise, the conditional becomes coincidental (اتفاقية) because there is no necessary relation between the antecedent and consequent. In contrast, with simple ignorance (الجهل البسيط) the conditional is necessary (لزومية).
This distinction is crucial: simple ignorance is the pure absence of knowledge, so its relation to ignorance is necessary.
4
Conclude: absences cannot coexist
Because knowledge and ignorance are opposites, the absence of one entails the presence of the other. Therefore, the two absences cannot coexist in the same locus. The opponent's objection fails.
The author thus defends the original position against the refined objection.

وما ذكرتموه من هذا القبيل، مع أنه غير تام في نفسه، وإليه أشار بقوله : ( وأيضاً : فلا نسلم اجتماع العدمين، إذ عدم العلم جهل، وعدم الجهل علم، وبينهما ) أي بين العلم والجهل (تضاد وتناف، فإن قلت : نحن نقول : لو جاز أن تكون العالمية معللة بعلم عدمي لجاز أن تكون الجاهلية معللة بجهل عدمي، فإذا اجتمع هذان العدميان في محل كان عالماً جاهلا بشيء واحد من جهة واحدة . قلت : لا نسلم أنه إذا كان مسمى العلم عدمياً وموجباً لكون محله عالماً كان الجهل مسمی أيضاً عدمياً موجباً لكون محله جاهلاً ، سلمناه لكن لا نسلم إمكان اجتماع هذين . قوله : (قلنا إلخ ) حاصله أنه فرق بين لا علم له وعلمه لا والنزاع في الثاني دون الأول. قوله : ( وأيضاً فلا نسلم اجتماع إلخ) يعني أن مقدم الشرطية أعني قوله فإذا عدما محال فيجوز أن يستلزم المحال إذ عدم كل منهما يستلزم وجود الآخر فلا يمكن اجتماع عدميهما. قوله: (وتناف) حمل التضاد على المعنى اللغوي ليتم التقريب إذ تحقق التضاد لا يقتضي امتناع ارتفاعهما بخلاف التنافي . قوله : (فإن قلت إلخ) تحرير للاستدلال المذكور بحيث يندفع المنعان. وحاصله الاستدلال بالعلم والجهل المركب يعني لو جاز تعليل العالمية بالعلم المعدوم لجاز تعليل الجاهلية بالجهل المركب المعدوم؛ إذ لا فرق بين العالمية والجاهلية لكون كل منهما حكماً ثبوتياً ولا بين عليتهما لكونهما معدومين، فإذا اجتمع هذان العدميان أي اتصف محل واحد بهما لزم كونه عالماً وجاهلاً معاً، فاندفع المنع الأول لاعتبار ثبوتهما لشيء واحد، والثاني لعدم كون أحدهما عدماً للآخر. قوله : ( قلت لا نسلم إلخ) حاصله : أنه حينئذ تكون الشرطية اتفاقية؛ إذ لا علاقة بين المقدم والتالي بخلاف ما إذا اعتبر الجهل البسيط، فإنها حينئذ تكون لزومية، كما عرفت . مع ورود المنع الثاني لأن العلم والجهل متقابلان وإن لم يكن أحدهما عدماً للآخر. قوله: (وأيضاً فلا نسلم اجتماع العدمين فيه بحث لأن الظاهر أن كلامهم في العلم والجهل المركب ويجوز اجتماع عدميهما. قوله : (تضاد وتناف) فسر التضاد بالتنافي الذي هو أعم ليمكن حمله على المذهبين وهما كون التقابل بينهما تقابل التضاد وتقابل العدم والملكة . قوله: (فإن قلت: نحن نقول إلخ) هذا إشارة إلى رد الجواب الأول بأنه ليس بصحيح إذ يمكن تقرير الكلام ،هكذا، وإلا فلا جهة له أصلاً لأن جوابه قد فهم، بل صرح به في قوله : وأيضاً نسلم إلخ .
And what you mentioned is of this kind, although it is not complete in itself. To it he gestured by his saying: (And also: we do not concede the coexistence of two absences, for the absence of knowledge is ignorance, and the absence of ignorance is knowledge, and between them) i.e., between knowledge and ignorance (there is opposition and conflict. If you say: we say: if it were permissible for worldliness (العالمية) to be caused by non-existent knowledge, then it would be permissible for ignorance (الجاهلية) to be caused by non-existent ignorance; then if these two absences coexist in a locus, it would be knowing and ignorant of one thing from one aspect. I say: we do not concede that when the name of knowledge is non-existent and causes its locus to be knowing, that ignorance is also named non-existent and causes its locus to be ignorant; if we concede that, we do not concede the possibility of the coexistence of these two. He said: (We said, etc.) Its summary: he distinguishes between 'he has no knowledge' and 'his knowledge is not', and the dispute is about the second, not the first. He said: (And also we do not concede, etc.) Means that the premise of the conditional, i.e., his saying 'when they are absent', is impossible, so it is permissible that impossibility follow, for the absence of each entails the existence of the other, so their two absences cannot coexist. He said: (And conflict) he interprets opposition (التضاد) in a linguistic sense to complete the approximation, for the realization of opposition does not necessitate the impossibility of their removal, unlike conflict (التنافي). He said: (If you say, etc.) It is a formulation of the aforementioned argument such that the two objections are repelled. Its summary: the argument is by composite knowledge and ignorance, i.e., if it were permissible for worldliness to be caused by non-existent knowledge, then it would be permissible for ignorance to be caused by composite non-existent ignorance, for there is no difference between worldliness and ignorance in that each is a positive judgment, nor between their causes in that both are non-existent. Then if these two absences coexist, i.e., one locus is described by both, it necessitates its being knowing and ignorant together. Thus the first objection is repelled because of considering their affirmation for one thing, and the second because one is not the absence of the other. He said: (I say we do not concede, etc.) Its summary: in that case, the conditional is coincidental, for there is no relation between the antecedent and consequent, unlike when simple ignorance is considered, for then it would be lizumiya (necessary), as you know, along with the occurrence of the second objection because knowledge and ignorance are opposites even if one is not the absence of the other. He said: (And also we do not concede the coexistence of two absences) There is discussion in it, because apparently their speech is about composite knowledge and ignorance, and it is permissible for their two absences to coexist. He said: (Opposition and conflict) He interpreted opposition as conflict in a broader sense to allow it to bear upon both doctrines: that the opposition between them is opposition of contraries and opposition of privation and possession. He said: (If you say: we say, etc.) This is a reference to the rejection of the first answer, that it is not correct, because the speech can be formulated thus, and otherwise there is no basis for it at all, because its answer has been understood, but he stated it in his saying: 'and also we concede, etc.'
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage continues the response to the objection from knowledge and ignorance. The author first notes that the opponent's example is not complete. He then introduces a further argument from the commentator: the phrase 'and also: we do not concede the coexistence of two absences' means that the absence of knowledge is ignorance and the absence of ignorance is knowledge, so knowledge and ignorance are opposites (تضاد وتناف). Therefore, they cannot both be absent from a locus, because the absence of one entails the presence of the other. The opponent then reformulates the objection using composite (مركب) knowledge and ignorance, claiming that if non-existent knowledge can cause worldliness, then composite non-existent ignorance can cause ignorance, and if both non-existent descriptions coexist in a locus, it would be both knowing and ignorant. The response is that even if we concede that composite ignorance can also cause ignorance, the conditional is coincidental (اتفاقية) because there is no necessary relation between the antecedent and consequent, unlike the case of simple ignorance (الجهل البسيط) which is the mere absence of knowledge. The commentator further explains that the opponents are discussing composite knowledge and ignorance, and it is possible for their absences to coexist. The discussion clarifies that the real issue is whether a privative attribute can be a cause, and the author carefully distinguishes different senses of absence.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a person can be neither knowing nor ignorant about something, i.e., that the absences of knowledge and ignorance can coexist.
Correction: The passage shows that knowledge and ignorance are opposites (tadad/tanaf), so the absence of knowledge is ignorance and the absence of ignorance is knowledge. Thus, one of them must always be present, and two absences cannot coexist.

Checkpoint

A) Because they are synonyms
B) To show that knowledge and ignorance are opposites such that the absence of one is the presence of the other
C) To prove that ignorance is existent
D) To reject the opponent's claim

Key Takeaway
Knowledge and ignorance are opposites, so the absence of one implies the presence of the other; therefore, two absences cannot coexist in the same locus.

Why a Non-Existent Attribute Cannot Cause a Positive Judgment
The author argues against a non-existent attribute causing a positive judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical reason why a non-existent attribute cannot serve as the cause (illa) for a positive judgment, based on the impossibility of contradictory attributes coexisting.

Core Idea
A non-existent attribute cannot cause a positive judgment without causing contradiction.

This passage delves into a subtle but crucial point in the theology of divine attributes: can a lack or absence of something be the cause of a positive quality? The author argues that this leads to absurd consequences—specifically, the possibility of a single subject possessing contradictory attributes. We'll walk through the argument, from the initial statement of the problem to the resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the gist of the argument.
The passage includes a statement of the issue, an objection, and a response.
2
Identify the two positions
Notice the author's statement of the issue: a subject described by a non-existent attribute that causes a positive judgment. He then rejects the alternative view as obviously false.
3
Trace the objection
Follow the opponent's reasoning: if non-existent knowledge causes being a knower, then non-existent ignorance would cause being ignorant.
4
Understand the contradiction
See how combining these leads to a subject being both knower and ignorant of one thing from one aspect.
This is the key consequence that makes the original position untenable.
5
Analyze the author's response
The author first denies the equivalence between knowledge and ignorance being both non-existent causes, and then even if conceded, denies the possibility of their combination.
The response relies on the opposition between knowledge and ignorance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يتصف محل بصفة عدمية ويكون ذلك موجباً لحكم ثبوتي في ذلك المحل، لا أنه يجوز أن تسلب صفة عن محل ويكون ذلك السلب موجباً له حكم تلك الصفة، فإنه ظاهر البطلان. وما ذكرتموه من هذا القبيل، مع أنه غير تام في نفسه، وإليه أشار بقوله : ( وأيضاً : فلا نسلم اجتماع العدمين، إذ عدم العلم جهل، وعدم الجهل علم، وبينهما ) أي بين العلم والجهل (تضاد وتناف، فإن قلت: نحن نقول : لو جاز أن تكون العالمية معللة بعلم عدمي لجاز أن تكون الجاهلية معللة بجهل عدمي، فإذا اجتمع هذان العدميان في محل كان عالماً جاهلا بشيء واحد من جهة واحدة . قلت : لا نسلم أنه إذا كان مسمى العلم عدمياً وموجباً لكون محله عالماً كان الجهل مسمی أيضاً عدمياً موجباً لكون محله جاهلاً ، سلمناه لكن لا نسلم إمكان اجتماع هذين
A subject is described by a non-existent attribute and that causes a positive judgment in that subject, not that it is permissible to negate an attribute from a subject and that negation causes the judgment of that attribute, for that is obviously false. What you have mentioned is of this kind, although it is not complete in itself, and to this he pointed by his saying: (Also: we do not concede the conjunction of two non-existents, for the absence of knowledge is ignorance, and the absence of ignorance is knowledge, and between them) i.e. between knowledge and ignorance (there is opposition and conflict. If you say: we say: if it were permissible for being a knower to be caused by a non-existent knowledge, then it would be permissible for being ignorant to be caused by a non-existent ignorance. So if these two non-existents are combined in a subject, it would be a knower and ignorant of one thing from one aspect. I say: we do not concede that if the meaning of knowledge is non-existent and causes its subject to be a knower, then ignorance is also non-existent and causes its subject to be ignorant. We concede that, but we do not concede the possibility of their combination.)
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is a statement of the problem concerning whether a non-existent attribute can be a cause (illa) for a positive judgment (hukm thubuti). The author begins by defining the issue: a subject is described by a non-existent attribute, and that causes a positive judgment. He immediately rejects the alternative—that the negation of an attribute could cause the judgment of that attribute—as obviously false. Then he references an objection from the opponent, which is introduced by 'his saying' (qawlihi). The objection argues that if a non-existent knowledge (ilm 'adami) could cause someone to be a knower (alim), then a non-existent ignorance (jahl 'adami) could cause someone to be ignorant. This would lead to a contradiction: the same subject would be both a knower and ignorant of the same thing. The author then gives the answer: first, he does not concede that if knowledge is non-existent and causes being a knower, then ignorance must also be non-existent and cause being ignorant. Even if that is conceded, he does not concede the possibility of their combination (ijtima'ahuma) because of the opposition between knowledge and ignorance. The argument thus shows that a non-existent attribute cannot be a cause, as it leads to impossible consequences.

العلة
المعلول
الصفة العدمية
الحكم الثبوتي
العدم
العلم
الجهل
التضاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A non-existent can have no causal power at all.
Correction: The debate is about a non-existent attribute causing a positive judgment; the argument shows that if it did, it would lead to contradiction, not that a non-existent cannot be a cause in any sense.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main flaw in allowing a non-existent attribute to cause a positive judgment?
A) It leads to the possibility of contradictory attributes in the same subject.
B) It violates the principle of non-contradiction regarding existence.
C) It confuses the negation of an attribute with the attribute itself.
D) It assumes that a non-existent can be a cause, which is impossible.

Key Takeaway
A non-existent attribute cannot cause a positive judgment because it would allow contradictory attributes like knowledge and ignorance to coexist in the same subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

When you attribute qualities to God, do you ever rely on mere negations (e.g., 'He is not unjust') without grounding them in positive affirmations from revelation? How does the author's insistence on avoiding contradictory non-existent causes challenge you to purify your understanding of God's attributes?
?
Think of a time you assumed a non-existent cause for a positive outcome in your life (e.g., 'I am successful because I am smart' when the evidence points elsewhere). How does this argument reveal the logical flaw in such assumptions, and how can you replace them with honest reflection?

Analysis of the Objection and the Meaning of Opposition
Commentator dissects the objection and clarifies the role of opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's step-by-step analysis of the objection concerning the relationship between knowledge and ignorance, and the precise meaning of opposition (tadadd) in this logical context.

Core Idea
Opposition (tadadd) here means mutual exclusion, not mere absence, allowing the argument to hold.

The commentator carefully dissects the previous objection, distinguishing between different types of negation and clarifying the role of opposition. This lesson will guide you through his detailed analysis, helping you grasp the logical moves and the importance of precise terminology in theological argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without overanalyzing. Then read the full English translation. Notice how the commentator breaks down each part of the objection.
The passage uses technical terms like shartiyya (conditional) and jahl murakkab (composite ignorance).
2
Identify the two preventions (man'ayn)
The commentator mentions two preventions in the original objection. Try to spot them in the text: one about the subject being described by both, and another about one being the absence of the other.
These are key to understanding the argument's structure.
3
Understand the reformulation with composite ignorance
The commentator rephrases the inference using 'composite ignorance' to show that if non-existent knowledge can be a cause, then non-existent composite ignorance can also be a cause, leading to a contradiction.
This is meant to repel the two preventions.
4
Grasp the accidental vs. necessary conditional
The response explains that the conditional becomes accidental (ittifaqiyya) when using composite ignorance, but necessary (luzumiyya) when using simple ignorance. This distinction is crucial.
It also shows the importance of the correct interpretation of opposition.
5
Reflect on the final refutation
The passage ends by indicating that there is no way to prove the possibility of combining the two non-existents, thus the objection fails.

قوله : (قلنا إلخ ) حاصله أنه فرق بين لا علم له وعلمه ،لا والنزاع في الثاني دون الأول. قوله : ( وأيضاً فلا نسلم اجتماع إلخ) يعني أن مقدم الشرطية أعني قوله فإذا عدما محال فيجوز أن يستلزم المحال إذ عدم كل منهما يستلزم وجود الآخر فلا يمكن اجتماع عدميهما. قوله: (وتناف) حمل التضاد على المعنى اللغوي ليتم التقريب إذ تحقق التضاد لا يقتضي امتناع ارتفاعهما بخلاف التنافي . قوله : (فإن قلت إلخ) تحرير للاستدلال المذكور بحيث يندفع المنعان. وحاصله الاستدلال بالعلم والجهل المركب يعني لو جاز تعليل العالمية بالعلم المعدوم لجاز تعليل الجاهلية بالجهل المركب المعدوم؛ إذ لا فرق بين العالمية والجاهلية لكون كل منهما حكماً ثبوتياً ولا بين عليتهما لكونهما ،معدومين، فإذا اجتمع هذان العدميان أي اتصف محل واحد بهما لزم كونه عالماً وجاهلاً معاً، فاندفع المنع الأول لاعتبار ثبوتهما لشيء واحد، والثاني لعدم كون أحدهما عدماً للآخر. قوله : ( قلت لا نسلم إلخ) حاصله : أنه حينئذ تكون الشرطية اتفاقية؛ إذ لا علاقة بين المقدم والتالي بخلاف ما إذا اعتبر الجهل البسيط، فإنها حينئذ تكون لزومية، كما عرفت . مع ورود المنع الثاني لأن العلم والجهل متقابلان وإن لم يكن أحدهما عدماً للآخر. قوله: (وأيضاً فلا نسلم اجتماع العدمين فيه بحث لأن الظاهر أن كلامهم في العلم والجهل المركب ويجوز اجتماع عدميهما. قوله : (تضاد وتناف) فسر التضاد بالتنافي الذي هو أعم ليمكن حمله على المذهبين وهما كون التقابل بينهما تقابل التضاد وتقابل العدم والملكة . قوله: (فإن قلت: نحن نقول إلخ) هذا إشارة إلى رد الجواب الأول بأنه ليس بصحيح إذ يمكن تقرير الكلام ،هكذا، وإلا فلا جهة له أصلاً لأن جوابه قد فهم، بل صرح به في قوله : وأيضاً نسلم إلخ . العدميين مع ما بينهما من التقابل ولا سبيل إلى الدلالة على هذا الإمكان أصلاً.
His saying: (We said etc.) Its summary is that he distinguished between 'he has no knowledge' (la ilma lahu) and 'his knowledge' (ilmuhu), and the dispute is about the second, not the first. His saying: (Also we do not concede the conjunction etc.) It means that the antecedent of the conditional, i.e. his saying 'so if they are non-existent' is impossible, so it may imply the impossible, since the nonexistence of each entails the existence of the other, so their two nonexistences cannot be combined. His saying: (and opposition) He interpreted opposition (tadadd) in the linguistic sense to complete the approximation, since the realization of tadadd does not preclude their elevation (irtifa') unlike conflict (tanafi). His saying: (For if you say etc.) It is a reformulation of the aforementioned inference such that the two preventions are repelled. Its summary is the inference by composite knowledge and ignorance, meaning: if it were permissible to explain being a knower (al-alamiyya) by non-existent knowledge, then it would be permissible to explain ignorance by composite non-existent ignorance; for there is no difference between being a knower and being ignorant since each is a positive judgment, nor between their two causes since they are both non-existent. So if these two non-existents are combined, i.e. a single subject is described by both, it would entail its being simultaneously a knower and ignorant. Thus the first prevention is repelled because they are considered existent for one thing, and the second because one is not the nonexistence of the other. His saying: (I say we do not concede etc.) Its summary: that in that case the conditional is accidental; for there is no relation between the antecedent and consequent, unlike when simple ignorance is considered, for then it would be necessary, as you have known. Along with the occurrence of the second prevention because knowledge and ignorance are opposites, even if one is not the nonexistence of the other. His saying: (Also we do not concede the conjunction of the two non-existents) In it there is a discussion because the apparent meaning is that their speech is about composite knowledge and ignorance, and it is permissible for their two non-existents to be combined. His saying: (opposition and conflict) He interpreted opposition as conflict (tanafi) which is more general to allow carrying it on the two schools, namely that the opposition between them is the opposition of contradiction and the opposition of privation and possession. His saying: (For if you say: we say etc.) This is a reference to the refutation of the first answer, that it is not correct, because the speech can be formulated like this, otherwise it has no basis at all, because his answer was understood, nay he explicitly said in his saying: 'and also we concede etc.' The two non-existents with the opposition between them, and there is no way to indicate this possibility at all.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the objection and answer from the previous lesson. It breaks down each part of the original argument. The commentator first clarifies the difference between 'he has no knowledge' (a negation of knowledge) and 'his knowledge' (the attribute of knowledge), noting that the dispute is about the latter. Then he explains that the opponent's conditional 'if they are non-existent' is impossible, so it may imply an impossible conclusion. The term 'opposition' (tadadd) is interpreted in the linguistic sense to make the argument work, because real tadadd does not prevent both from being absent, unlike conflict (tanafi). The commentator then reformulates the inference using composite knowledge and ignorance (al-jahl al-murakkab) to show that if non-existent knowledge can be a cause, so can non-existent composite ignorance, leading to a subject being both knowing and ignorant. This repels the two previous preventions. The response is that then the conditional is accidental, not necessary, because there is no real relation between the antecedent and consequent unless simple ignorance is considered. Finally, the commentator notes that there is a discussion about whether the two non-existents can combine, but the apparent meaning is that they are talking about composite entities, so their non-existents can combine. The passage ends by stating that there is no way to prove the possibility of that combination.

Key Terms
الشرطية
التضاد
التنافي
الجهل البسيط
الجهل المركب
المنع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that opposition (tadadd) in this context means simple logical contradiction (e.g., true/false) or that it necessarily implies one is the absence of the other.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that tadadd here is taken in the linguistic sense of mutual exclusion (tanafi), which is broader and does not require one to be the absence of the other.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator say about the conditional in the objection?
A) It is a necessary conditional.
B) It is an accidental conditional with no real relation between antecedent and consequent.
C) It is impossible because the antecedent is false.
D) It is a tautology.

Key Takeaway
The commentator shows that interpreting opposition as mutual exclusion (tanafi) and distinguishing simple from composite ignorance are crucial to understanding the logical dynamics of the objection.

In your own life, have you ever treated your ignorance about a matter of faith as a mere absence when it was actually a positive rejection?
